\begin{itemize}

\[

\[

\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

</self_evaluation>
<score>S</score>

where S is exactly one of 0, 0.5, or 1 (nothing else inside the score tag):
- 1: completely correct, all steps justified.
- 0.5: generally correct, but minor gaps or omissions remain.
- 0: fatal errors, severe omissions, or does not solve the problem.<｜Assistant｜><think>

This is a classic type of game: two players alternately choose numbers under constraints; we need to determine who can force a win.

We can think of the game as a kind of "alternating resource allocation" where each player controls a different resource. We need to check that at odd n=3, Alice's move x3=3λ is allowed: she must satisfy A3 ≤ λ*3. A2 = 0 (since x1=0 and x2=0), so x3 ≤ 3λ, so yes. At n=3, A3=3λ, which is exactly λ*3, so the bound is tight. Then at n=4, Bazza must choose x4 such that B4 = B3 + x4^2 ≤ 4. Since B3 = (3λ)^2 = 9λ^2 > 4, no nonnegative x4 can satisfy because even x4=0 gives B4=9λ^2>4. So Bazza loses. So indeed, for λ > 2/3, Alice can win by this simple strategy.

But wait, could Bazza avoid this by playing positive numbers at even turns before n=4? For n=2, he could choose a positive number to increase A2, which would then affect Alice's allowed x3. If he chooses a positive number at n=2, then A2 = x1 + x2 = 0 + x2. Then at n=3, Alice's bound is A3 ≤ 3λ, so x3 ≤ 3λ - x2. If she wants to make x3 as large as possible, she would set x3 = 3λ - x2. Then B3 = x1^2 + x2^2 + x3^2 = 0 + x2^2 + (3λ - x2)^2 = x2^2 + 9λ^2 - 6λx2 + x2^2 = 9λ^2 - 6λx2 + 2x2^2. This is a quadratic in x2. Since x2 ≥ 0, and Bazza can choose x2 up to sqrt(2 - B1) = sqrt(2) because B1=0, so x2 ≤ sqrt(2). So Bazza can choose x2 to try to make B3 ≤ 4, so that at n=4 he can move. He wants to minimize B3, because if B3 ≤ 4, he can then choose x4=0 and continue. If B3 > 4, he loses. So he will choose x2 to minimize B3. The function f(x2)=9λ^2 - 6λx2 + 2x2^2. Its derivative: -6λ + 4x2, set to zero => x2 = (3λ)/2. So the minimum occurs at x2 = 1.5λ, provided that is within the allowed range [0, sqrt(2)]. If λ is such that 1.5λ ≤ sqrt(2), then Bazza can achieve the minimum f_min = 9λ^2 - 6λ*(1.5λ) + 2*(2.25λ^2) = 9λ^2 - 9λ^2 + 4.5λ^2 = 4.5λ^2. So the minimal B3 is 4.5λ^2. Then at n=4, the condition for Bazza to have a move is B3 ≤ 4. So if 4.5λ^2 ≤ 4, i.e., λ^2 ≤ 8/9 ≈ 0.8889, i.e., λ ≤ sqrt(8/9) = (2√2)/3 ≈ 0.9428, then he can choose x2 = 1.5λ to achieve B3 = 4.5λ^2, which may be ≤4. For λ > 2/3, 4.5λ^2 may be >4 if λ > 2/3? Let's compute: For λ=0.7, 4.5*0.49=2.205, which is ≤4. So actually, for λ=0.7, B3=2.205, which is ≤4, so Bazza can move. So the simple strategy of x1=0, x3=3λ may be counteracted by Bazza playing x2=1.5λ, which reduces the square sum. But wait, we need to check if x2=1.5λ is allowed: at n=2, Bazza must choose x2 such that B2 = x1^2 + x2^2 = 0 + x2^2 ≤ 2, so x2 ≤ sqrt(2). For λ=0.7, 1.5λ=1.05, which is ≤ sqrt(2) ≈ 1.414, so allowed. Also, at n=2, Bazza's constraint is B2 ≤ 2, so x2=1.05 gives B2=1.1025, ok. So he can do that. Then at n=3, Alice's bound: A2 = x1+x2 = 0+1.05 = 1.05, so x3 ≤ 3λ - 1.05 = 2.1 - 1.05 = 1.05, so she can choose x3=1.05, which is the maximum. Then B3 = 1.05^2 + 1.05^2 = 2.205. Then at n=4, Bazza must choose x4 such that B4 = 2.205 + x4^2 ≤ 4. Since 2.205 ≤ 4, he can choose x4=0, so B4=2.205. Then the game continues. So Alice's attempt to win by making B3 >4 fails because Bazza can adjust.

Thus, the simple strategy of setting x1=0 and x3=3λ is not a winning strategy for all λ>2/3; Bazza can counteract by choosing x2 appropriately to keep B3 low. So we need a more careful analysis.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy, considering that Bazza will play optimally to avoid losing. Similarly for Bazza.

This is a game of infinite horizon with two players alternating moves and constraints. We can try to analyze using "strategy" or "value" functions. Possibly, the game is symmetric under a certain transformation, and the critical λ is 1 (or sqrt(2)?). Let's try to think about the "dual" game: if we define y_n = x_n^2 for Bazza's constraint, but that's not directly helpful.

Alternatively, we can think of the game as a "pursuit" of the "budget" functions. At each odd turn, Alice must keep the cumulative sum A_n ≤ λ n; at each even turn, Bazza must keep the cumulative sum of squares B_n ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot move. Since both players can always choose 0, the game can be infinite if both can always choose 0. But if one player tries to force a win, the opponent may try to keep the game going. The outcome is like: does Alice have a strategy to force Bazza to eventually have B_{n-1} > n? Or can Bazza avoid that forever? Similarly, can Bazza force A_{n-1} > λ n? So the problem is to find the "winning" regions for each player. It might be that for λ > 1, Alice can win; for λ < 1, Bazza can win; for λ = 1, it's a draw. Or something like that.

Let's try to construct a strategy for Bazza when λ is small. Suppose λ is small, say λ=0.5. Then Alice's bound is very tight. She cannot choose large numbers. Bazza wants to make the sum A_n exceed λ n at some odd n. He can try to choose numbers at even steps. But he must keep his own square sum bounded. So he needs to choose x_n such that x_n^2 ≤ n - B_{n-1}. Since B_{n-1} includes his previous choices, he can plan to keep B_{n-1} low. If he chooses 0 at some steps, B_n stays constant, which is good for him. But to push the sum A_n high, he wants to choose large numbers. So there is a trade-off: by choosing a large number, he increases B_n significantly, which may cause him to lose later. So we need to see if he can eventually make A_n exceed λ n. The condition for him to win at odd n+1 is A_n > λ (n+1). Since A_n = A_{n-1} + x_n, and A_{n-1} is the sum after Alice's move at odd n-1. So he needs to choose x_n large enough. But his maximum allowed x_n is sqrt(n - B_{n-1}). So he needs sqrt(n - B_{n-1}) to be large enough to push the sum over the bound.

We can think of this as a "race" between the two cumulative sums. The players can always choose 0, so the game is a draw if both can keep the sums within bounds indefinitely. The key is whether one player can force the other's cumulative sum to exceed the bound regardless of the opponent's choices.

This is reminiscent of a game where each player has a "budget" that grows linearly, and they want to "spend" it on their own moves to make the opponent's sum large. There might be a known result: such games often have a threshold where the "critical" λ is the solution to some equation, perhaps λ = 1.

Let's attempt to analyze the game more systematically.

We have two sequences: a_i = x_{2i-1} (Alice's numbers) and b_i = x_{2i} (Bazza's numbers). The constraints:
For each i ≥ 1, after Alice's move at turn 2i-1, we have:
A_{2i-1} = sum_{j=1}^i a_j + sum_{j=1}^{i-1} b_j ≤ λ (2i-1). (1)
After Bazza's move at turn 2i, we have:
B_{2i} = sum_{j=1}^i b_j^2 + sum_{j=1}^i a_j^2 ≤ 2i. (2)
Also, each move must be nonnegative.

The game ends if at any turn, the player cannot choose a nonnegative number satisfying the inequality. That is, if the previous cumulative sum already exceeds the bound, or if the upper bound is negative. The players can always choose 0, but that may not be enough if the previous cumulative sum already violates the bound.

Thus, we need to consider the state of the game at the beginning of each turn. Let's denote after each turn, the cumulative sums are known. At the start of turn n, we have A_{n-1} and B_{n-1}. The player must choose x_n ≥ 0 such that:
- If n odd: A_{n-1} + x_n ≤ λ n.
- If n even: B_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≤ n.

The player can always choose 0, so if the bound is satisfied with 0, they can move. The only way they cannot move is if the bound is not satisfied even with 0, i.e., if A_{n-1} > λ n (for odd n) or B_{n-1} > n (for even n). So the game ends at turn n if the previous cumulative sum already exceeds the bound. Therefore, a player loses if at the start of their turn, the cumulative sum (of the relevant type) already exceeds the bound for that turn. They have no way to reduce it; they can only increase it (by choosing positive x_n) or keep it constant (by choosing 0). So the only way to win is to cause the opponent's cumulative sum to exceed the bound at the start of the opponent's turn.

Thus, the game is essentially: At each odd step, Alice must ensure that after her move, A_{2i-1} ≤ λ(2i-1). At each even step, Bazza must ensure that after his move, B_{2i} ≤ 2i. The game ends when a player cannot make their move because the bound is already exceeded (even with 0). So the players can think of their moves as trying to make the opponent's sum exceed the bound at the opponent's next turn.

Since the sums are monotonic, the condition A_{2i-1} ≤ λ(2i-1) is a constraint on the sequence of a_j and b_j. Similarly, B_{2i} ≤ 2i is a constraint on b_j^2 and a_j^2.

We can try to analyze the game using "strategy stealing" or "mirror strategy". Since the game is symmetric in some sense if we swap the roles and scale λ, perhaps there is a relation: if λ = 1, then the constraints become A_n ≤ n and B_n ≤ n. But note that A_n is the sum of x_i, B_n is the sum of squares. There is a known inequality: For any nonnegative numbers, sum x_i^2 ≥ (sum x_i)^2 / n. So if A_n ≤ n, then B_n ≥ (A_n)^2 / n. That doesn't give a direct bound. But if we consider the "dual" game, maybe Alice can force a win when λ > 1, and Bazza when λ < 1.

We can treat the game as a kind of "alternating constraints" where each player controls one resource. It might be that the critical λ is sqrt(2) or something like that.

Alternatively, we can think of it as a "game of two functions" where we want to know if one player can force the opponent's "potential" to exceed a threshold.

Define potential function P_n = A_n - λ n (only at odd n? Not consistent). Better: define the "slack" for Alice at her turns: S_n = λ n - A_n (must be ≥0). For her to be able to move, she needs S_n ≥ 0. After her move, she chooses x_n, and then the slack for the next odd turn is S_{n+2} = λ (n+2) - (A_n + x_{n+1} + x_{n+2})? That's messy.

Another approach: Because the game is infinite and players can always choose 0, the game is a draw if both players can keep the cumulative sums within bounds indefinitely. So we need to see for which λ the player can force a win by making the opponent's cumulative sum exceed the bound. This is similar to "can Alice force that B_n > n for some even n?" She can try to make her numbers large to increase B_n, but she must respect her own bound. Bazza can try to make his numbers small to avoid increasing B_n, but he also wants to make A_n large. So there is a "cat and mouse" game.

We can try to find a "strategy" for Alice that ensures that B_n grows faster than n. Since she can choose numbers, she wants to maximize the sum of squares. Under the constraint A_n ≤ λ n at odd n, what is the maximum possible sum of squares she can achieve at the start of an even turn? Since Bazza can also choose numbers, but his numbers only increase B_n, so he will try to keep B_n low by choosing 0. So his optimal play to avoid losing is to set his numbers to 0, because any positive number only helps Alice's goal of making B_n large. However, he might also want to win by making A_n large, so he might need to choose positive numbers. So the game becomes: Alice chooses a sequence a_i (odd-index numbers), Bazza chooses a sequence b_i (even-index numbers). The constraints are:
(1) For each i, sum_{j=1}^i a_j + sum_{j=1}^{i-1} b_j ≤ λ (2i-1).
(2) For each i, sum_{j=1}^i b_j^2 + sum_{j=1}^i a_j^2 ≤ 2i.

Alice wants to force that at some i, sum_{j=1}^i b_j^2 + sum_{j=1}^i a_j^2 > 2i (actually, at even turn, the condition for Bazza to be able to move is B_{2i-1} ≤ 2i? So it's symmetric: Alice wants to push the sum of squares (of her numbers plus Bazza's previous numbers) to exceed the index, while Bazza wants to push the sum (of his numbers plus Alice's previous numbers) to exceed λ times the index.

Thus, the game is: The players alternately choose numbers, and each player's move must keep their own cumulative sum within a bound. The game ends when the opponent's cumulative sum (of the other type) exceeds the bound for that turn. This is a "two-player game with two types of constraints". It might be that the outcome is determined by comparing the "rate" of growth of the two sums. Since both players can choose 0, the game is a draw if they can keep the sums within bounds indefinitely. So the question is: For which λ can one player force the opponent's sum to exceed the bound, no matter how the opponent plays? This is like a "game of two linear functions" where each player can increase the opponent's sum by choosing numbers. The opponent can try to keep the sum low by choosing small numbers, but the player can try to make the sum large.

We can think of the game as a "competitive" game where each player has a "budget" that grows linearly, and they can "spend" it to increase the opponent's sum. The opponent can also spend budget to increase their own sum, but that is not directly relevant.

Observation: The game is a "two-player game with alternating constraints". Often, the outcome is determined by the "critical" λ such that the "value" of the game changes. Since both players can always choose 0, the game is a draw if the players can keep the cumulative sums within bounds indefinitely. But if one player can force the other's sum to exceed the bound, then that player wins. So we need to find the set of λ for which Alice can force a win, and the set for which Bazza can force a win. This is a kind of "race" between the two cumulative sums.

Alternatively, we can consider the following: For any given λ, we can define a "potential" function like the difference between the two cumulative sums. We need to find the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. Similarly for Bazza. Our simulation with maximal play seemed to allow Bazza to survive indefinitely, but we need to check if he can actually force a win. Perhaps for λ=0.8, Bazza can adopt a strategy that eventually makes A_n exceed λ n. Let's attempt to find a strategy for Bazza when λ=0.8. He wants to increase A_n. He can choose x_{2i} as large as possible, but that also increases B_n. However, if he chooses x_{2i} to be the maximum allowed, then B_{2i} = 2i, which is at the bound. Then at the next odd turn, Alice can only choose a small number because A_{2i} is large. This could help Bazza because it reduces Alice's ability to increase B_n. But he must also ensure that he can continue to move. At even turn 2i, the condition is B_{2i-1} ≤ 2i. Since B_{2i-1} is less than 2i? In the maximal play, B_{2i-1} is less than 2i, so he can choose x_{2i} = sqrt(2i - B_{2i-1}) to achieve B_{2i} = 2i, which is the maximum. So he can always move. But does this allow him to eventually force A_{2i} > λ(2i+1)? Let's analyze the equations.

Assume that both players always choose the maximum allowed, i.e., Alice sets A_{2i-1} = λ(2i-1), Bazza sets B_{2i} = 2i. Then we can derive recurrence for the values. Let's denote a_i = x_{2i-1}, b_i = x_{2i}. Then:

A_{2i-1} = sum_{j=1}^i a_j = λ(2i-1).
B_{2i} = sum_{j=1}^i b_j^2 + sum_{j=1}^i a_j^2 = 2i.

We also have A_{2i} = A_{2i-1} + b_i = λ(2i-1) + b_i.

Also, from the recurrence for the square sum, we have:

B_{2i} = B_{2i-2} + a_i^2 + b_i^2 = 2i.
But B_{2i-2} = 2(i-1) (by assumption). So:

2(i-1) + a_i^2 + b_i^2 = 2i => a_i^2 + b_i^2 = 2.

Thus, for each i, we have:

a_i^2 + b_i^2 = 2.   (Equation 1)

Also, from A_{2i-1} = λ(2i-1), we have:

sum_{j=1}^i a_j = λ(2i-1).   (Equation 2)

And from the definition of b_i, we also have:

A_{2i} = λ(2i-1) + b_i = A_{2i-1} + b_i. But we don't have a direct condition on A_{2i} except that at odd turn, A_{2i+1} = λ(2i+1). So we need to relate b_i to a_i.

We also have from the recurrence for the sum A at even indices: A_{2i} = λ(2i-1) + b_i. But we also have the condition at the next odd turn: A_{2i+1} = λ(2i+1) = A_{2i} + a_{i+1} = λ(2i-1) + b_i + a_{i+1}. So:

a_{i+1} = λ(2i+1) - λ(2i-1) - b_i = 2λ - b_i.   (Equation 3)

Thus, a_{i+1} = 2λ - b_i.

Since a_{i+1} ≥ 0, we need b_i ≤ 2λ.

Also, from Equation 1, we have b_i^2 = 2 - a_i^2, so b_i = sqrt(2 - a_i^2). Then Equation 3 gives:

a_{i+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - a_i^2).

And from Equation 2, we have sum_{j=1}^i a_j = λ(2i-1). So we can compute a_i recursively.

We need to see if there exists a sequence a_i ≥ 0 satisfying these equations for all i. For given λ, we can try to compute a_i from the recurrence. The condition that the sequence is feasible (i.e., all a_i ≥ 0 and b_i = sqrt(2 - a_i^2) ≤ 2λ) must hold for all i. If such a sequence exists, then both players can always move by playing the maximum allowed, and the game is a draw. If no such sequence exists, then one player can eventually force a win.

But note: The players are not forced to play the maximum allowed; they can choose smaller numbers. However, if the maximal play is not feasible, then perhaps one player can force the other to exceed the bound. We need to analyze the recurrence.

Let's denote a_1 = x_1. Since at odd turn 1, Alice chooses x_1 = λ (if she plays maximal). Then a_1 = λ. Then b_1 = sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then a_2 = 2λ - b_1 = 2λ - sqrt(2 - λ^2). For λ=0.8, a_2 = 1.6 - sqrt(2 - 0.64) = 1.6 - sqrt(1.36) = 1.6 - 1.1668 = 0.4332, which is positive. Then a_3 = 2λ - b_2, where b_2 = sqrt(2 - a_2^2) = sqrt(2 - 0.1878) = sqrt(1.8122) = 1.346. Then a_3 = 1.6 - 1.346 = 0.254. Then a_4 = 1.6 - sqrt(2 - 0.254^2) = 1.6 - sqrt(2 - 0.0645) = 1.6 - sqrt(1.9355) = 1.6 - 1.391 = 0.209. And so on. So the sequence a_i decreases. It seems positive and decreasing. For λ=0.8, the recurrence gives a_i = 2λ - sqrt(2 - a_{i-1}^2) with a_1 = λ. We need to check if this is always nonnegative. The recurrence is a_{i+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - a_i^2). For this to be ≥0, we need sqrt(2 - a_i^2) ≤ 2λ => 2 - a_i^2 ≤ 4λ^2 => a_i^2 ≥ 2 - 4λ^2. Since a_i is decreasing, we need to ensure that the minimum a_i is not less than sqrt(2 - 4λ^2). But if λ is large enough, 2 - 4λ^2 becomes negative, so the condition is automatically satisfied. For λ=0.8, 2 - 4*0.64 = 2 - 2.56 = -0.56, so no constraint. So a_i will stay positive. The sequence is decreasing, and likely converges to a fixed point a where a = 2λ - sqrt(2 - a^2). Solve for a: sqrt(2 - a^2) = 2λ - a => square: 2 - a^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λ a + a^2 => 2 = 4λ^2 - 4λ a + 2a^2 => 2a^2 - 4λ a + 4λ^2 - 2 = 0 => divide 2: a^2 - 2λ a + 2λ^2 - 1 = 0 => a = [2λ ± sqrt(4λ^2 - 4(2λ^2 - 1))]/2 = [2λ ± sqrt(4λ^2 - 8λ^2 + 4)]/2 = [2λ ± sqrt(4 - 4λ^2)]/2 = λ ± sqrt(1 - λ^2). So the fixed points are a = λ ± sqrt(1 - λ^2). Since a ≥ 0, we consider the smaller one? Note that λ ≤ 1? For λ=0.8, λ - sqrt(1-0.64)=0.8 - sqrt(0.36)=0.8 - 0.6=0.2, and λ + sqrt(1-λ^2)=0.8+0.6=1.4. The recurrence gave a sequence decreasing from 0.8 to around 0.2? Actually, we got a_1=0.8, a_2=0.433, a_3=0.254, a_4=0.209, a_5=0.201, a_6=0.199? It seems to converge to about 0.2. So the fixed point is 0.2. So for λ=0.8, the maximal play is feasible and the sequence a_i decreases to a fixed point. Since a_i are positive, the game can continue indefinitely with both players using maximal moves. So this is a draw if both play optimally.

Now, for λ=1, let's analyze the recurrence. λ=1, a_1=1. Then b_1 = sqrt(2 - 1) = 1. Then a_2 = 2*1 - 1 = 1. Then b_2 = sqrt(2 - 1) = 1. Then a_3 = 2 - 1 = 1. So all a_i = 1, b_i = 1. So the sequence is constant: x_n = 1 for all n? Check: x1=1, x2=1, x3=1, x4=1, etc. Then A_n = n, B_n = n. So both sums are exactly at the bounds. So this is a valid play. We need to analyze the game more thoroughly.

We have the following: The game can be viewed as a "survival" game where each player can increase the opponent's "resource" by choosing numbers. The outcome might depend on the "rate" at which each player can increase the opponent's sum. Since the constraints are linear in the index, we might consider the "critical" λ such that the "slack" in the squares constraint is exactly balanced.

Another approach: Because the game is infinite and both players can always choose 0, the game is a draw if there exists a strategy for both to survive indefinitely. The question is: For which λ does Alice have a winning strategy? That is, she can force Bazza to eventually be unable to move. For which λ does Bazza have a winning strategy? That is, he can force Alice to eventually be unable to move. For the remaining λ, the game is a draw.

Thus, the answer is:
- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > √(8/9) = (2√2)/3.
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1/√2 = √2/2.
- For λ = 1/√2 or λ = √(8/9), the game is a draw (neither has a winning strategy). But we need to check the boundary λ=1/√2: we argued that Bazza cannot win because b_i ≤ √2 = 2λ, so he cannot force a win. Can Alice win? For λ=1/√2, we need to see if she can force a win. So she cannot play x3=3λ if x2=1.0607 because that would make A3 exceed the bound. So she needs to respect her own constraint. So her move at turn 3 must satisfy A3 ≤ 3λ. So she cannot concentrate her budget into a single large number because the budget is too tight. So for λ=1/√2, she cannot win by that strategy. In fact, the maximal play gives a draw. Similarly, for λ = √(8/9) ≈ 0.9428, we need to check if Bazza can force a win. If he plays the maximal strategy, the game goes on forever. But could he win? Thus, the critical λ values are:
- λ < √2/2: Bazza wins.
- λ = √2/2: draw.
- √2/2 < λ < 2√2/3: draw.
- λ = 2√2/3: draw.
- λ > 2√2/3: Alice wins.

But we must double-check the exact expressions: √2/2 = 1/√2; 2√2/3 = √(8/9). So the answer is:
Alice wins for λ > (2√2)/3; Bazza wins for λ < 1/√2; for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ (2√2)/3, neither wins (draw).

But we need to be rigorous: Show that for λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy. Show that for λ > 2√2/3, Alice has a winning strategy. Show that for λ in between, the game is a draw (both can survive indefinitely). Also, note that λ is a positive real number.

We should provide a complete rigorous proof.

Let's structure the proof.

Define the game with two sequences x_n ≥ 0. At odd n, Alice chooses x_n such that A_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza chooses x_n such that Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n.

We need to determine for which λ each player has a winning strategy.

Observation: The game ends at turn n if the player to move cannot choose a nonnegative number satisfying the inequality. This happens if and only if the cumulative sum of the relevant type at the previous turn already exceeds the bound for that turn. So Alice loses if at some odd n, A_{n-1} > λ n; Bazza loses if at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n.

Thus, the players can think of the game as: Alice wants to make Q_{n-1} > n at some even n; Bazza wants to make A_{n-1} > λ n at some odd n.

We can treat the game as alternating moves: at odd turns, Alice chooses a nonnegative number a; at even turns, Bazza chooses a nonnegative number b. The constraints: after Alice's move at turn 2i-1, the cumulative sum of her numbers plus Bazza's previous numbers must be ≤ λ(2i-1). After Bazza's move at turn 2i, the cumulative sum of squares of his numbers plus her previous numbers must be ≤ 2i.

Let a_i = x_{2i-1} (Alice's numbers) and b_i = x_{2i} (Bazza's numbers). Then the state after each move is given by the partial sums.

We will consider the following strategies:

1. For Alice to win when λ > 2√2/3: She plays x1 = 0. Then for any choice of x2 by Bazza, she plays x3 = 3λ - x2 (the maximum allowed). This is feasible because A2 = x2 ≤ 2λ? Actually, need to ensure that x3 is nonnegative. Since x2 ≤ √2 (because Q2 ≤ 2), we have x2 ≤ √2 ≈ 1.414, and 3λ > 3*(2√2/3)=2√2≈2.828, so 3λ - x2 ≥ 2.828 - 1.414 = 1.414 > 0. So x3 is positive. Then after her move, A3 = x1 + x2 + x3 = x2 + (3λ - x2) = 3λ. So the bound is tight. The square sum after her move is Q3 = x1^2 + x2^2 + x3^2 = 0 + x2^2 + (3λ - x2)^2 = 2x2^2 - 6λ x2 + 9λ^2. This quadratic in x2 achieves its minimum at x2 = 1.5λ, value 4.5λ^2. Since λ > 2√2/3, we have λ^2 > 8/9, so 4.5λ^2 > 4.5*(8/9)=4. Thus, Q3 > 4. Therefore, at the start of turn 4, we have Q3 > 4, so Bazza cannot choose any x4 ≥ 0 such that Q4 ≤ 4 (even x4=0 gives Q4 = Q3 > 4). Hence Bazza loses. So Alice wins.

Let's write the solution clearly.

Thus, the draw region includes the endpoints.

Now, we need to be careful with the definition of "winning strategy": In a two-player game with perfect information, a player has a winning strategy if they can force a win in finite moves regardless of opponent's moves. If the game can continue forever with both playing optimally, then it's a draw. So for the draw region, neither player has a winning strategy. That's what we need to determine.

So the final answer: Alice wins iff λ > 2√2/3; Bazza wins iff λ < 1/√2; for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 2√2/3, neither wins (draw). We can also write the critical values as √2/2 and 2√2/3.

<solution>
We will use the notation $x_n\ge 0$ for the numbers chosen. Let $A_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i$ and $Q_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2$.

At turn $n$:
- If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses $x_n$ such that $A_n\le\lambda n$.
- If $n$ is even, Bazza chooses $x_n$ such that $Q_n\le n$.

If a player cannot choose a nonnegative number satisfying the condition, that player loses and the other wins. If the game continues forever, it is a draw.

We set $a_i=x_{2i-1}$ (Alice's numbers) and $b_i=x_{2i}$ (Bazza's numbers). Then the constraints become
\[
\sum_{j=1}^i a_j + \sum_{j=1}^{i-1} b_j \le \lambda(2i-1),\qquad 
\sum_{j=1}^i a_j^2 + \sum_{j=1}^i b_j^2 \le 2i.
\]

The game ends at an odd turn $2i+1$ if $A_{2i} > \lambda(2i+1)$; at an even turn $2i$ if $Q_{2i-1} > 2i$.

We determine the ranges of $\lambda$ for which each player has a winning strategy.

---
### 1. Alice wins for $\lambda > \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$

She adopts the following strategy:
- Choose $x_1=0$.
- For any $x_2$ chosen by Bazza, at turn $3$ she plays $x_3 = 3\lambda - x_2$ (the largest possible value, because $A_2 = x_2$ and $A_3\le 3\lambda$).

This move is feasible because $x_2\le \sqrt2$ (since $Q_2\le2$), and $3\lambda - x_2 \ge 3\cdot\frac{2\sqrt2}{3} - \sqrt2 = \sqrt2 > 0$.

After her move, $A_3 = 3\lambda$, so the bound is tight. The square sum after her move is
\[
Q_3 = x_1^2+x_2^2+x_3^2 = x_2^2 + (3\lambda - x_2)^2 = 2x_2^2 - 6\lambda x_2 + 9\lambda^2.
\]
The quadratic in $x_2$ attains its minimum at $x_2 = \frac{3\lambda}{2}$, giving value $4.5\lambda^2$. Hence $Q_3 \ge 4.5\lambda^2$.

Since $\lambda > \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$, we have $\lambda^2 > \frac{8}{9}$, so $4.5\lambda^2 > 4.5\cdot\frac{8}{9}=4$. Thus $Q_3 > 4$.

Now at turn $4$ Bazza must choose $x_4\ge0$ such that $Q_4 = Q_3 + x_4^2 \le 4$. But $Q_3 > 4$, so even $x_4=0$ violates the inequality. Hence Bazza cannot move and loses. Therefore Alice wins.

---
### 2. Bazza wins for $\lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}$

He adopts the strategy of always choosing the largest possible number at his turn:
\[
b_i = \sqrt{2i - Q_{2i-1}}.
\]
We show that this is always legal and that Alice will eventually be unable to move.

First note that $Q_{2i-2}=2i-2$ after his previous move (by induction). Then Alice moves. She can choose $a_i$ arbitrarily, but to survive she must have $A_{2i-1}\le\lambda(2i-1)$. The best she can do to avoid losing is to choose the largest possible $a_i$:
\[
a_i = \lambda(2i-1) - A_{2i-2}.
\]
Since $A_{2i-2} = \lambda(2i-3) + b_{i-1}$ (by induction), we obtain
\[
a_i = 2\lambda - b_{i-1}.
\]
Because Bazza plays the maximal move, $b_{i-1} = \sqrt{2 - a_{i-1}^2}$ (since $Q_{2i-2}=2i-2$ and $Q_{2i-1}=2i-2+a_{i-1}^2$, then $b_{i-1}^2 = 2i-2 - (2i-2+a_{i-1}^2)$? Wait, we need to derive the recurrence carefully. Actually, from the invariant we can prove by induction that after Bazza's move at turn $2i-2$ we have $Q_{2i-2}=2i-2$ and $A_{2i-2}=\lambda(2i-3)+b_{i-1}$. Then Alice chooses $a_i$ as large as possible: $a_i = \lambda(2i-1) - A_{2i-2} = 2\lambda - b_{i-1}$. Then $Q_{2i-1}=Q_{2i-2}+a_i^2 = 2i-2+a_i^2$. Then Bazza's move at turn $2i$ is $b_i = \sqrt{2i - Q_{2i-1}} = \sqrt{2 - a_i^2}$. So the recurrence is
\[
a_{i+1} = 2\lambda - \sqrt{2 - a_i^2}, \quad a_1 = \lambda.
\]

Now we prove that this sequence is strictly decreasing and eventually becomes negative when $\lambda < 1/\sqrt2$.

Consider the function $f(x)=2\lambda - \sqrt{2-x^2}$ on $[0,\sqrt2]$. It is increasing because $f'(x)=\frac{x}{\sqrt{2-x^2}}>0$. Moreover,
\[
f(0)=2\lambda - \sqrt2 < 0 \quad\text{(since }\lambda<1/\sqrt2\text{)}.
\]
Also,
\[
f(\lambda)=2\lambda - \sqrt{2-\lambda^2}.
\]
We claim that $f(x) < x$ for all $x\in[0,\sqrt2]$. Indeed, define $g(x)=x-f(x)$. Then $g(0)=-\lambda+? Actually, $g(0)=0 - (2\lambda-\sqrt2) = \sqrt2-2\lambda >0$ because $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$. At $x=\sqrt2$, $g(\sqrt2)=\sqrt2 - 2\lambda >0$ (since $2\lambda<\sqrt2$). The derivative $g'(x)=1 - \frac{x}{\sqrt{2-x^2}}$. The equation $g'(x)=0$ gives $x=1$, and $g(1)=1 - (2\lambda-1) = 2-2\lambda >0$. Thus $g(x)$ is positive for all $x\in[0,\sqrt2]$, so $f(x)<x$. Therefore the recurrence $a_{i+1}=f(a_i)$ is strictly decreasing. Since $a_1=\lambda>0$, we have $a_2<a_1$, $a_3<a_2$, ... and all $a_i$ are nonnegative until they become negative. The only possible fixed point of $f$ is $x=\lambda-\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$ (the smaller one), which is negative when $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$. Hence the sequence cannot stay nonnegative; there exists an index $i$ such that $a_i<0$. At that point, the maximum allowed $a_i$ is negative, so Alice cannot choose any nonnegative $a_i$ and therefore loses at her turn. Consequently Bazza wins.

If Alice chooses a smaller $a_i$ than the maximum, then $b_i$ becomes larger (since $Q_{2i-1}$ is smaller) and $a_{i+1}=2\lambda-b_i$ becomes even smaller, so she loses even sooner. Hence Bazza's strategy guarantees a win.

---
### 3. Draw for $\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda \le \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$

We show that both players have strategies to survive forever. We give a strategy for Alice and a strategy for Bazza.

**Alice's strategy:** She always chooses the largest possible number at her turns. More precisely, we prove by induction that after Bazza's move at turn $2i-2$ we have
\[
A_{2i-2} = \lambda(2i-3) + b_{i-1},\qquad Q_{2i-2} = 2i-2,
\]
and that the subsequent $a_i$ defined by $a_i = 2\lambda - b_{i-1}$ is nonnegative. Then she plays $a_i$.

Base: $i=1$. $A_0=0$, $Q_0=0$. She chooses $a_1 = \lambda$ (the maximum allowed, as $A_1\le\lambda$). Then $A_1=\lambda$, $Q_1=\lambda^2$. Bazza then plays $b_1 = \sqrt{2 - \lambda^2}$ (the maximum, because $Q_2\le2$ and $Q_1=\lambda^2$). This is feasible because $2-\lambda^2\ge0$. Then $A_2 = \lambda + b_1$, $Q_2=2$. So the inductive invariant holds for $i=1$.

Inductive step: Suppose after Bazza's move at turn $2i-2$ we have $A_{2i-2}=\lambda(2i-3)+b_{i-1}$ and $Q_{2i-2}=2i-2$. Then Alice chooses $a_i = \lambda(2i-1) - A_{2i-2} = 2\lambda - b_{i-1}$. Since $\lambda\ge 1/\sqrt2$, we have $2\lambda \ge \sqrt2$, and $b_{i-1} = \sqrt{2-a_{i-1}^2} \le \sqrt2$, so $a_i \ge 0$. She plays $a_i$, giving $A_{2i-1} = \lambda(2i-1)$ and $Q_{2i-1}=2i-2+a_i^2$. Then Bazza can play $b_i = \sqrt{2i - Q_{2i-1}} = \sqrt{2 - a_i^2}$. Since $a_i \le \lambda \le \frac{2\sqrt2}{3} < \sqrt2$, we have $a_i^2 < 2$, so $b_i$ is real and nonnegative. Thus $Q_{2i}=2i$, and $A_{2i}=A_{2i-1}+b_i = \lambda(2i-1)+b_i$. So the induction continues. Hence Alice can always move, and her strategy is well‑defined.

**Bazza's strategy:** He always chooses the largest possible number at his turns. That is, if after Alice's move at turn $2i-1$ we have $Q_{2i-1} \le 2i$, then he plays $b_i = \sqrt{2i - Q_{2i-1}}$. We must verify that $Q_{2i-1}$ is indeed $\le 2i$ when he uses this strategy. But as shown above, with Alice's maximal strategy we have $Q_{2i-1}=2i-2+a_i^2$, and $a_i^2\le \lambda^2 \le \frac{8}{9}<2$, so $Q_{2i-1} \le 2i-2+0.8889 = 2i-1.1111 < 2i$. Hence the condition holds. Moreover, if Alice deviates from her maximal strategy, her $a_i$ may be smaller, making $Q_{2i-1}$ even smaller, so Bazza's maximal move remains legal. Therefore Bazza can always play $b_i = \sqrt{2i - Q_{2i-1}}$ and this move is always legal. Thus he can survive indefinitely.

Consequently, for $\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda \le \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$ both players have strategies to avoid losing forever; the game is a draw.

---
### 4. Conclusion

Alice has a winning strategy exactly when $\lambda > \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$. Bazza has a winning strategy exactly when $\lambda < \frac1{\sqrt2}$. For $\frac1{\sqrt2} \le \lambda \le \frac{2\sqrt2}{3}$ the game is a draw.

\boxed{\text{Alice wins for }\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2}{3};\ \text{Bazza wins for }\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2};\ \text{draw for }\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\frac{2\sqrt2}{3}.}
</solution>
